рд╢реНрд░реАрд░рд╛рдорджреЗрд╢рд┐рдЧрджрд╛рд╕рдйреНредINTRODUCTION Drред Vред Varadacharya (French Institute of Indology, Pondicherryред) The outlook of the Hindus has been intensely religious from immemorial timesред The sources for the beliefs and concepts of the Hindu religion cannot be tracedред It wlll not therefore be wrong to treat them as beginninglessред The creeds and sects of the Hindu religion have been varied on their practical aspect but have common threads connecting them together and keeping them intact within the Hindu foldред The deities that get worship at the hands of the people are many but among them Vi┼Я┼Жu and Siva have a high degree of prominence over others, as is evidenced by available literary, epi- graphical and sculptural sourcesред Vedanta, however avers that Vi┼Яnu is the higher among the twoред Religious faith, which has thus sprung from sources not ascertainable by us, have found the need to be safeguarded for maintaining stabilityред This was achieved by the philosophical doctrines which again had to pass through different currentsред The Upanisads played a dominant role in evolving principles and concepts to pursue the true path in search of Ultimate Reality which became identified with Vissn or Sivaред The epics Vi┼Яnu and Puranas were brought in to support and explain the doctrinal tenets in the light of the teaching of the Upanisadsред The Upanisads on a cursory reading would appear not to contain any thing like one central doctrineред The seers had their vision of Reality and taught the same to their pupilsред The pupils, in their turn, transmitted what they had learnt from their masters, to their own pupils and in so doing had to take note of the expositions offered by other teachers to the same or other doctrinesред The pupils turned teachers are not found to use the very same expressions, which are used by their preceptors and/or by contempo- rariesред The variations in the denotative power of such expressions became the starting point for the beginning of a system of thought with a name new and different from that of the earlier periodsред Variations in the expositions inevitably led to the need for asserting one’s exposition as correct and controverting those of others as unacceptable and thus unauthoritative tooред All these were recorded at first in the commentaries written by the early scholars on standard texts called Sutrasред These commentaries con- tain an exposition of the central doctrines and also refutations of those opposed to themред [t ii All the Bh─Бс╣гyas, Varttikas and others could be cited as illustrationsред Along with these, treatises were written by the writers for their schools, on one or more fundamental doctrines, furnishing evidence in support and also for rejecting those unacceptable to themред In this atmosphere of scholastic treatment, there arose a tendency to write a compendium giving an exposition of the doctrines of many systems and schools of thoughtред Works of this kind could be grouped under different classesред To the first class belong those works which are fairly impartial while detailing the tenets of the schoolsред The Agamaс╕Нambara of Jayantabhaс╣нс╣нa, Saddar┼Ыana samuccaya of Haribhadrasuri and Manameyarahasya┼Ыlokav├дrttika of Laksmipuram Srinivasacharya belong to this groupред The works, belonging to the second class, reveal their authors’ preference for the doctrines which they hold and also show that other schools receive their due treat- This class has the following works: рез) Sarvasiddh├дntasamgraha of Samkara реи) Sarvadarsanasiddhanta samgraha of Samkara, рей) Tattvamuktakal├дpa with the Sarv├дrthasiddhi of Vedanta De┼Ыika, рек) Sarvatantra┼Ыiromani of R─Бm─Бc─Бrya рел) Sarva- matasarasamgraha of Damodara and рем) Manameyodaya of N─Бr─Бyaс╣Зabhaс╣нс╣нaред To the third class belong works which state openly the preferences of their authors to their doctrines, through exposing the defects in other systems and schoolsред They are рез) Tattvasamgraha of Santarakс╣гita with the Pa├▒jik├д commentary by Kamala- sila, реи) Sarvadar┼Ыanasamgraha of Cennubhaс╣нс╣нaрез рей) Saddar┼Ыana siddh├дntasamgraha of Ramabhadradik┼Яita and рек) Sarvamatasiddh├дntadipika of Sarv─Бtmasambhu and рел) Sarvadar┼Ыanakaumudi of Madhavasarasvatiред The last class eomprises of works which aim at the condemnation of the schools which are opposed to their authorsред The aim of the writers of these works should have- been to expose the weakness of the rival schools, thereby proving that their systems are formidable and are on stable groundsред The titles of such works contain the wordsред bhanga, khanda, bhanjana, dusani and othersред It is curious to note that Jayarasibhatta wrote the Tattvopaplavasinha demons- trating that any tenet or concept could be refused mercilessly thus giving the supreme резред Cennubhaс╣нс╣нa mentions this as his work in his commentary on Varadaraja’s- T├дrkikarakс╣г─Бред iii place to Vitanda (futile rejoinder)ред His approach seems to be nearer Mahavidya- syllogism, though not identical with itред His method is emulated by Sriharsa who wrote the Khandanakhandakhadya with devastating logical acumen and did not establish any thing, though his intention was to place the system of Advaita on stable groundsред Vedanta Desika’s, Paramatabhanga and Satadusani, Pancamatabhanjana of Tatadesika and Bahuvidhamatakhandana of unknown authorship come under the last classред Schools other than Vedanta also have works like the Pramanyavadakhandava, Satakotikhandava, Vidhirasayananirsana and othersред Visi┼Яс╣н─Бdvaita is the name of the school of Vaisnavism in which the philoso- phical concepts and religious principles are woven inextricably togetherред Apart from the Bhasyas on the Brahmasutras, Git├д and others, specific attention is to be drawn to the Nyay atattva of Nathamuni and Siddhitraya of Yamunaред These works contain an exposi- tion of the doctrines of the school along with the refutation of the rival theoriesред Though Ramanuja’s Sribhasya is of a similar kind, his Ved├дrthasamgraha enjoys a unique posi- tion as it contains a methodical treatment of the principles of Visiс╣гс╣нadvaita and a refu- tation of the opponents’ theoriesред Par─Б┼Ыarabhaс╣нс╣нa’s Tattvaratn─Бkara, Varadan─Бr─Бyaс╣Зa- bhaс╣нс╣н─Бraka’s Praj├▒aparitr─Бс╣Зa, and Meghanadari’s Nayadyumani and Atreya Ramanuja’s Nyayakulisa reveal the same approachред It is in Vedanta De┼Ыika, a polymath, that one finds all aspects of scholastic skill from the writing of simple manuals to that of most abstruse logical treatises on Vaisnavismред His Nyayapari┼Ыuddhi, Nyayasiddha├▒jana and Se┼Ыvararamim├дms├д are like those of early writers in matter and formред The V├дditrayakha с╣Зс╕Нana, Satad┼лsaс╣Зi and Paramatabhanga suggest the vigour and force of his arguments levelled against the rivalsред The first mentioned work among these, refutes the theories of Samkara, Bhaskara ane Yadavaprak─Б┼Ыa and the second contains a critical review of the doctrines of the Advaitinsред His reasons reveal his logical acumenред The last mentioned work offers a reasoned estimate of the rival schools, followed by a refutation of themред The Tattvamuktakalapa and Paramatabhanga have a close likeness in contain- ing the treatment of the doctrines of all schoolsред Treatment of them is of course diffe- rentред In the former work, the chapters have headings like dravya, buddhi, nayaka, jiva and othersред The concepts of all the schools under each head are treated and re- futedред Thus it is an encyclopaedia of the Visiс╣гс╣нadvaita school of Vedantaред On the other hand, the Paramatabhanga contains chapters each devoted to set forth the doctrines of one particular system or school, followed by a critcism of themред шо╕ ф╕А d iv The Paramatabhanga was written after Se┼Ыvaramim├дms├д and Satad├╝┼Яaniред Regarding the place where релремaред) vii The The purpose of writting this work is given in two waysред The commentator of this edition notes (on Pредрей) that he got the information from Vidvan Sri Ayya Krishna Tatacharya of Kanci that one Govindanayanar of Sikkal entered in to a discussion with Sri De┼Ыika at Ka├▒ci and continued the discussion at Tiruvahindrapuram to which place Sri De┼Ыika wentред This disputant got satisfied with the arguments of Sri De┼Ыika and went awayред The work was then written by the author at Tiruvahindrapuramред second version is contained, on Pред реиреореи of the edition of this work by Sri Sред Sред Aiyangarред According to this, the exponents of the rival schools were defeated earlier, by Sri De┼Ыika at Srirangamред They came one after the other to Tiruahindrapuram were Srih Desika was staying, and had their debates with the author and got defeated at his handsред Both these do not to appear to be acceptableред One single scholar could not have argued in defence of all the rival schoolsред Nor is there evidence to show that there were as many exponents as there are systems of thought stated in this work as were refuted by Sri De┼Ыikaред A close study of this work shows that the rival schools: (Sections VI-XXI) refuted here are sixteenред Among these, it is unthinkable that there were exponents and adherents, eyen in the резрекth century, for the Lok├дyata (VI), Bhaskara (XIII) and Vaiya- V karana (XIV)ред There must have been a single system of Buddhism treated in four sections VII-Xред The same must be said of the Nyaya, Vai┼Ыe┼Яika, Samkhya, Yoga and M├нm├дms├д schools dealt with in sections XV - XIX, which however have been having academic protoganistsред There was at no time, any particular school professing anti- Pa├▒car─Бtra viewsред Thus the probable position should have been that the systems men- tioned here could have been of two groupsред One group consisted of the scholars adред hering to and professing certain doctrines like Buddhist, Jaina and Pasuapataред The other group comprised of the academic exponents of various systems and schools of thoughtред Secondly, it is quite improbable that all these rival groups came at one time or in succession to Tiruvahindrapuram for a combat with Sri De┼Ыikaред Certainly they did comeред It may be all or only some and at this date, it is impossible to identify or determine their numberред It will be prudent to take most of the systems and schools of thought mentioned here as simply systems of thought followed by adherents for academic purposesред Why did the author adopt the maniprav├дla (Sanskrit blended with Tamil) mode for writing this work? The disputants particularly of the Nyaya, Vaiseс╣гika, Mim├дms├д, viii Samkhya, Yoga, Bhaskara and Vaiyakarana systems, must have used only Sanskrit while debating with the authorред It is unthinkable that Tamil expressions were used by themред While recording the full course of the debates, the author must have chosen the mani- pravala way of writingред The technical words of Sanskrit origin would not be readily comprehended, if they were rendered into Tamilред Besides, the tenets of schools along with their refutation are already found treated by the author in his Sanskrit treatises like Satad┼лс╣гaс╣З─л, Tattvamukt─Бkalapa, Nyayapari┼Ыuddhi, Nyayasiddh├д├▒jana, Se┼Ыvaramim─Бm s─Б and othersред As in the Rahasyatrayasara, the author desired those people who could follow Sanskrit in a Bhartс╣Ыhari (Chapter XIV)ред In the first chapter of Introduction, the author offers obeisance to God and states that the tenets of the system should be established by its adherents after refuting those of the rivalsред Tradition should be respected and on no account be floutedред ix The second section, which comprises Chapters II to IV, contains a concise account of the self, concept of the ┼Ыloka indi- cates that no one other than Ved├дnta De┼Ыika could have written a work of this kindред The school of Vaisnavism, an developed by Ramanuja, has been seeking to preach its doctrine to people of the scholarly kind and also of the ordinary typeред The former has good grounding in Sanskrit and the necessary equipment to understand the treatment of the matters presented by the writers in the Sastraic terminologyред Hence the ancient works were written in Sanskritред Besides, the doctrines dealt with in Sanskrit by the writers in other S├дstras and systems of thought are also required to be known in order to follow what the Vaisnava doctrines areред With the exception of very few scholars who have this kind of equipment to read and discuss the writings in Sanskrit, people, as such, have only a general understanding of the sense of words of Sanskrit originред They do not possess the knowledge of the Sastrasред They are to be provided with such facilities as are required to get them familiar with the doctrines of Vaisnavismред For such peopleред the Vaisnava school adopted the manipravala mode of presenting the doctrinesред Sanskrit and Tamil got blended with each other in thisред One of them is treated as a gem and the other as a coralред The two get a happy blendingред The matters that get treated through them, are of easy comprehension to one and all, including the scholars who are learned in the Sastrasред Hence many works were written by this method since Ramanuja’s periodред xiii Here again, there are two kinds of works written in this methodред One is of purely religious kind in the background of philosophical significance and the other is mainly of philosophical matterред The Rahasyatrayasara of Vedanta Desika which is mainly in Tamil with Sanskrit words of common occurrence represents the former kindред The Paramatabhanga perhaps the only work of its kind, represents the second kindред The author’s aim in this work is to present, in each chapter, the fundamental doctrines of a particular system and then refute themред It is necessary therefore to men- tion all the doctrines of that system with utmost brevityред All of them are to be stated in due orderред This cannot be done except by using the Sanskrit terms which could not be satisfactorily rendered into Tamilред Tamil is used generally as a link between the Sanskrit expressions to make senseред Refutation of these doctrincs is required to be done in a similar wayред In such cases the crisp Sanskrit expressions are not easily compre- hendedред It will be a difficult and futile effort to render the sense of these technical terms in Tamilред Rather, there must be a very elaborate explanation and exposition of these in Tamilред This effort, even if undertaken, will not serve the purpose of making people, not learned in the Sastras, understand the principles and argumentsред The fact is that even eminent scholars find it difficult to grasp the purposes served by the use of some of these expressionsред They too require explanations for themред Sanskrit happens to be the only medium to make them understand the full implications of such usagesред The problem here is that no attempt has so far been made to offer even explanatory notes for the different passages found in this workред Considering all these hardships that require to be faced while offering exposi- tions for this work in Tamil, the commentator Vedantav├дvad ┼лka Villivalam N─Бr─Бyaс╣З─Б- charya Svami chose the right mode of rendering the expositions for the entire work in Sanskritред The language used by the commentator is simple, forceful and purposefulред The most hard technical expressions of Sanskrit origin are made easy of comprehensionред Not one word or phrase, not one passage or doctrine is ignored by himред Explanations are very elaborate in certain cases and sufficiently serve the purpose without the least tinge of monotonyред It is hard to find a single case in which he offers his argument and explanation without sufficient authority citing it from various sourcesред Besides citing the passages from the works of writers who are well-known, the author had uti- lised his knowledge of the works of leading exponents in other schools as well, as is clear from his referencesред Mention could be made of Kaiyata (Chapter рей, Pред резреирей), реиремреи), V├дdike┼Ыav├дsrui (Chapter рей, Pред резреирем) and Vij├▒─Бnabhikс╣гu (Chapter рен, Pред репрек), all in Part I, of the bookред It is not possible to single out any particular passage as having not received adequate explanation at the hands of the commentatorред At the outset, the author refers to his uudertaking the work where the rival theories are refuted and his own stand is establishedред These two are essential to determine the the nature of Reality (Part I, pред рео-резреж)ред On this, the commentator observes that both have to get adoptedред Of course, even when one of them is undertaken, a general knowledge of the rival schools becomes known since the other also gets included hereред Yet the subtle points could not be graspedред If the opposite view is not refuted, then those rival theories may be taken to be on a par with one’s own theoriesред If one’s the- ories are not sought to be established, then our ancient writers are likely to be taken as having hurled futile rejoinders at the rival schools as in the work (Khandan a Khanda Khadya) of Khanс╕Нanakara Sri Har┼Яaред Hence the objections have to be ans- wered so as to enable one’s own system develop without any criticism from the rivals (Pред резрез)ред The author writes that the self is the subordinate (┼Ыe┼Яa) to Godред To this an objection is notedред How can a self which does not take to any activity for personal pur- poses be considered as subordinate to another iредeред God?ред The author answers that the servants work to gain their own ends and yet are found to be subordinates to kings and othersред (P, рейрен)ред The commentator makes this clear by observing that there is no- thing contradictory, if a person takes to an activity at one time to gain personal ends and to achieve a purpose at another timeред The servant takes to activity by rendering service to the master to become happy by getting the wages for the service renderedред Yet this happiness gets included in the happiness caused by him to his master through his service for the latter, So it is clear that such servants, though working for personal benefits, are really working to bring joy to the mastersред Yet, a difference deserves to be noted hereред While working for the master, personal benefit is prominent in the ser- vant and the master’s getting the result of this service is incidentalред When the servant takes to work for personal gain, it is the other way about viz, pleasing the master is prominent and working for personal benefit is incidentalред So when the real self un- dertakes a work for a personal end, that purpose is connected with the purpose to be ac- quired by the master and so the latter alone is prominent in such a caseред Hence the self who is a servant becomes a subordinate (sesa) to God (Pред рейрен)ред While pointing out the fallacies in the arguments of the nihilists who do not admit anything as real, the author remarks that the nihilist does not admit difference XV between pram├гс╣Зa and apram─Б┼Жaред Then he must be admitting what he treats as worth- less to be like the flower in the sky-a non existent phenomenonред He must hold that all cognitions have no objective content like the dream cognitions (ppред резрезрео-резрезреп)ред The commentator observes that the Buddhists (nihilists) admit jar and others as having existence when reference is made to themред They have to admit that there is no hair on the shell of the tortoise, since admission of this is sublatedред The nihilist does not need any prama┼Жa to establish nihilismред If prama┼Жas are admjtted, then nihilism will not i get establishedред As every thing is a void, there is no object to be proved to existред This is thus stated in the Khandana Khanda Khadyaред The disputants hold as a rule while entering into debates, that prama┼Жas and the objects are admitted by allред Others do not subscribe to this conceptред Since what is authoritative and what is not cannot be classi- fied in the nihilistic school, the nihilists cannot escape from admitting that there is no difference between what is held to be real and what is notред An alternate explanation is offered here by the commentatorред The word pram─Бс╣З─Бpramana can be taken as pram─Б┼Жa and wrong prama┼Жaред That is, there can be no difference between a pram─Б┼Жa and what is a defective pram─Б┼Жaред Then sublation is not possible for the unauthoritative by the authoritativeред Then no one position in any argument could be admittedред The commen- tator cites a passage from the author’s Sarv├дrthasiddhi in support of his interpretationред The commentator then takes up an interesting objection to answerред The nihi- list does not of course admit a difference between pram─Б┼Жa and apram─Б┼Жa but admits that difference for maintaining empirical realityред At least inference is admitted to be pram─Б┼Жa for this purposeред (Chapter рен, PPред резрезреп-резреиреж)ред under his father, he studied for the Nyaya Siromani course at Tirupathi and passed the examination in резрепрейреп with a creditable First Classред He studied Vedanta under Hред Hред Srirangasathakopayatindramahadesika, the рекреиnd Head of the Ahobila Muttред He served the Ahobila Mutt College at Madurantakam for реирек years (резрепрекреи-резрепремрем) and the Varanaseeya Visvavidyalaya, Kasi for ten years (резрепремрем-резрепренрел)ред He took a leading part in all gatherings and annual meets of Vidv─Бns, particularly held under the Malola Vidvat Sadasред To the misfortune of one and all, who had great respect and admiration for his erudition, silent work, and unassuming temperament, he passed away in резрепренреоред He is not alive to see his contribution to Visist├дdvaita published as contained in this edition of Paramatabhangaред Yet, we are happy that his contribution has been carried to print to remind us constantly of his greatnessред ред*ред
